      S UBSPACE O PTIMIZATION FOR L ARGE L ANGUAGE
002   M ODELS WITH C ONVERGENCE G UARANTEES

009                                               A BSTRACT
010
011            Subspace optimization algorithms, with GaLore (Zhao et al., 2024) as a repre-
012            sentative method, have gained popularity for pre-training or fine-tuning large lan-
013            guage models (LLMs) due to their memory efficiency. However, their conver-
014            gence guarantees remain unclear, particularly in stochastic settings. In this paper,
015            we unexpectedly discover that GaLore does not always converge to the optimal
016            solution and substantiate this finding with an explicit counter-example. We then
017            investigate the conditions under which GaLore can achieve convergence, demon-
018
               strating that it does so either in deterministic scenarios or when using a suffi-
               ciently large mini-batch size. More significantly, we introduce GoLore (Gradient
019
               random Low-rank projection), a novel variant of GaLore that provably converges
020
               in stochastic settings, even with standard batch sizes. Our convergence analysis
021            can be readily extended to other sparse subspace optimization algorithms. Finally,
022            we conduct numerical experiments to validate our theoretical results and empiri-
023            cally explore the proposed mechanisms.
024
025
026   1     I NTRODUCTION
027
028   Large Language Models (LLMs) have demonstrated impressive performance across a variety of
029   tasks, including language processing, planning, and coding. However, LLMs require substantial
030   computational resources and memory due to their large model size and the extensive amounts of
031   training data. Consequently, recent advancements in stochastic optimization have focused on de-
032   veloping memory-efficient strategies to pre-train or fine-tune LLMs with significantly reduced com-
033   puting resources. Most approaches (Vyas et al., 2024; Ramesh et al., 2024; Luo et al., 2023; Liu
034
      et al., 2024; Bini et al., 2024; Hao et al., 2024; Zhao et al., 2024; Muhamed et al., 2024; Pan et al.,
      2024; Loeschcke et al., 2024; Hayou et al., 2024; Lialin et al., 2023; Han et al., 2024; Song et al.,
035
      2023) concentrate on reducing the memory of optimizer states, which are critical components of
036
      overall training memory consumption. For instance, optimizers such as Adam (Kingma, 2014) and
037   AdamW (Loshchilov, 2017) maintain first and second-order momentum terms for gradients as opti-
038   mizer states, leading to significant memory overhead for large models.
039
040
      Among the most popular memory-efficient fine-tuning algorithms is LoRA (Hu et al., 2021), which
      decreases the number of trainable parameters by employing low-rank model adapters. However, the
041
      low-rank constraint on weight updates can result in substantial performance degradation for tasks
042
      that require full-rank updates, particularly in the pre-training of LLMs. To address this issue, sev-
043   eral LoRA variants have been proposed, including ReLoRA (Lialin et al., 2023) and SLTrain (Han
044   et al., 2024). Recently, GaLore (Zhao et al., 2024) has emerged as an effective solution, significantly
045   reducing optimizer states by projecting full-parameter gradients into periodically recomputed sub-
046   spaces. By retaining optimizer states in low-rank subspaces, GaLore can reduce memory usage by
047   over 60%, enabling the pre-training of a 7B model on an NVIDIA RTX 4090 with 24GB of memory.
048   In contrast, the vanilla 8-bit Adam without low-rank projection requires over 40GB of memory.
049
050   1.1   F UNDAMENTAL OPEN QUESTIONS AND MAIN RESULTS
051   While GaLoreвҖҷs memory efficiency has been well established both theoretically and empirically, its
052   convergence guarantees remain unclear. This raises the following fundamental open question:
053
                Q1. Can GaLore converge to stationary solutions, under regular assumptions?

      Figure 1: Loss curves of algorithms using AdamW (left) and Momentum SGD (right) on problem (1), where

066
      By stationary solutions, we refer to first-order stationary points x вҲҲ Rd such that вҲҮf (x) = 0 for
067
      objective function f : Rd вҶ’ R. By regular assumptions, we refer to standard conditions in non-
068
      convex smooth optimization, including lower boundedness, L-smoothness and unbiased stochastic
069   gradients with bounded variances, as outlined in Assumptions 1-3 in Sec. 2.
070
071   Contrary to expectations, our investigation reveals that GaLore does NOT converge to stationary
072
      solutions under regular assumptions. The intuition behind this finding is straightforward: GaLore
      projects the stochastic gradient matrix onto a low-rank subspace spanned by the top r singular vec-
073
      tors obtained via Singular Value Decomposition (SVD), effectively capturing the dominant compo-
074
      nents of the stochastic gradient matrix. However, the stochastic gradient comprises two components:
075   the true gradient and gradient noise. When the true gradient dominates, the SVD-identified subspace
076   primarily captures the gradient component. In contrast, as the algorithm approaches a local mini-
077   mum so that the true gradient diminishes while noise persists, the SVD-derived subspace captures
078   only the noise component, rather than the true gradient, ultimately leading to non-convergence. To
079   validate this intuition, we construct a counter-example demonstrating that GaLore fails to converge
080   to stationary solutions, see the illustration in Fig. 1. This leads us to a subsequent open question:
081
082         Q2. Under what additional assumptions can GaLore converge to stationary solutions?
083
084
      Based on the preceding discussion, we conclude that the SVD-identified subspace in GaLore aligns
      well with the descent direction in scenarios where the true gradient component dominates the gra-
085
      dient noise component. This observation naturally leads to two additional assumptions under which
086
      GaLore can converge:
087
088     вҖў Noise-Free Assumption. We theoretically establish that GaLore converges at a rate of O(1/T )
089       in the deterministic and non-convex setting.
090
        вҖў Large-Batch
                вҲҡ        Assumption. We theoretically demonstrate that GaLore converges at a rate of
091
          O(1/ T ) in the stochastic and non-convex setting, provided that the batch    вҲҡ size is extremely
092
          large and increases with the number of iterations T , e.g., a batch size of Оҳ( T ).
093
094
      However, neither the noise-free assumption nor the large-batch assumption applies to the practical
095
      pre-training and fine-tuning of LLMs. This leads to another fundamental open question:
096
097       Q3. Under what modifications can GaLore provably converge in the LLM setting, in which
098                gradient noise presents and the batch-size cannot be extremely large?
099
100   It is evident that SVD-based projections cannot extract meaningful information from noise-dominant
101   matrices. To address this issue, this paper proposes modifying the SVD projection to a Gradient
102
      Random Low-Rank projection, resulting in the GoLore algorithm for pre-training or fine-tuning
      LLMs. This random projection can effectively capture gradient information even when gradient
103
      noise predominates, allowing for convergence in the stochastic andвҲҡnon-convex setting with normal
104
      batch sizes. We establish that GoLore converges at a rate of O(1/ T ) under standard assumptions.
105
106   In our empirical experiments, we implement GaLore during the primary phases of pre-training or
107   fine-tuning LLMs due to its efficacy in capturing the gradient component using SVD-based projec-
      tion. In contrast, we employ GoLore in the final phase, leveraging its ability to extract the gradient


                                                                                    2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      component from noise-dominant stochastic gradients using random projection. This approach en-
109   hances performance compared to employing GaLore throughout all stages.
110
111   While our analysis primarily focuses on GaLore, it also has significant connections to other memory-
112
      efficient algorithms. We demonstrate that a ReLoRA-like implementation is equivalent to GaLore,
      which is more computational efficient with little additional memory overhead. Furthermore, our
113
      theoretical results can be easily adapted to sparse subspace descent algorithms with minimal effort.
114
115   Contributions. Our contributions can be summarized as follows:
116
        вҖў We find that GaLore cannot converge to stationary solutions under regular assumptions. The key
117
          insight is that the SVD-derived subspace primarily captures the noise component rather than the
118       true gradient in scenarios where gradient noise predominates. We validate the non-convergence
119       of GaLore by providing an explicit counterexample. This addresses Question Q1.
120
        вҖў Inspired by the aforementioned insight, we propose different additional assumptions under
121       which GaLore can provably converge to stationary solutions. Under the noise-free assump-
122       tion, we establish that GaLore converges at a rate of O(1/T ). Under the large-batch assumption
123       or some вҲҡadditional isotropic noise assumptions, we demonstrate that GaLore converges at a rate
124       of O(1/ T ). This addresses Question Q2.
125
        вҖў In settings where gradient noise persists and the batch size cannot be extremely large, we modify
126       the SVD projection in GaLore to a random projection, resulting       in the GoLore algorithm that
127
                                                                        вҲҡ
          provably converges to stationary solutions at a rate of O(1/ T ). This addresses Question Q3.
128
        вҖў We present an equivalent yet more computationally efficient, ReLoRA-like implementation of
129
          GaLore/GoLore, and extend our analysis to other sparse subspace descent algorithms.
130
        вҖў We conduct experiments across various tasks to validate our theoretical findings. In particular,
131
          by alternately using GaLore and GoLore during different phases in LLMs pre-training and fine-
132
          tuning, we achieve enhanced empirical performance.
133
134
      1.2 R ELATED WORK
135
136
      Memory-efficient training. In LLM training, the primary memory consumption arises not only
      from the model parameters but also from activation values and optimizer states. Jiang et al. (2022)
137
      and Yu et al. (2024) have proposed methods to compress activation values into sparse vectors to
138
      alleviate memory usage. Other approaches primarily focus on reducing optimizer states. A notable
139   work, LoRA (Hu et al., 2021) reparameterizes the weight matrix W вҲҲ RmГ—n as W = W0 + BA,
140   where W0 вҲҲ RmГ—n remains frozen as the pre-trained weights, and B вҲҲ RmГ—r and A вҲҲ RrГ—n
141   are learnable low-rank adapters. Variants of LoRA, such as those proposed by Liu et al. (2024)
142   and Hayou et al. (2024), aim to enhance training performance. However, constrained to low-rank
143   updates, LoRA and its variants are primarily effective for fine-tuning tasks and struggle with pre-
144   training tasks that require high-rank updates. To address this limitation, ReLoRA (Lialin et al.,
145   2023) enables high-rank updates by accumulating multiple LoRA updates, while LISA (Pan et al.,
146   2024) learns full-parameter updates on dynamically selected trainable layers. GaLore (Zhao et al.,
147
      2024) and F LORA (Hao et al., 2024) achieve high-rank updates by accumulating low-rank updates
      in periodically recomputed subspaces, and SLTrain (Han et al., 2024) employs additional sparse
148
      adapters for high-rank updates. SIFT (Song et al., 2023) also utilizes sparse updates. Although
149
      these algorithms have demonstrated comparable empirical performance to full-parameter training
150   methods, theoretical guarantees regarding their convergence have not been established. A recent
151   study by Liang et al. (2024) provides a proof of continuous-time convergence for a class of online
152   subspace descent algorithms, however, its analysis depends on the availability of true gradients rather
153   than the stochastic gradients that are more practical in LLM training. To the best of our knowledge,
154   this work offers the first analysis of the discrete-time convergence rate for memory-efficient LLM
155   training algorithms in stochastic settings.
156   Convergence for lossy algorithms. Many optimization algorithms utilize lossy compression on
157   training dynamics, such as gradients, particularly in the realm of distributed optimization with com-
158   munication compression. Researchers have established convergence properties for these algorithms
159   based on either unbiased (Li et al., 2020; Li & RichtaМҒrik, 2021; Condat et al., 2024; He et al.,
160   2024b;a; Mishchenko et al., 2019; Gorbunov et al., 2021; Alistarh et al., 2017; He et al., 2023) or
161   contractive (RichtaМҒrik et al., 2021; Xie et al., 2020; Fatkhullin et al., 2024; He et al., 2023) com-
      pressibility. Kozak et al. (2019) provides a convergence analysis for subspace compression under


                                                        3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      Polyak-Lojasiewicz or convex conditions, where the subspace compression adheres contractive com-
163   pressibility at each iteration. Despite these extensive findings, analyzing the convergence properties
164   of subspace descent algorithms like GaLore remains challenging, as the compressions used can be
165   neither unbiased nor contractive due to the reuse of projection matrices.
166

230   3    N ON - CONVERGENCE OF G A L ORE : I NTUITION AND C OUNTER -E XAMPLE
231
232   In this section, we demonstrate why GaLore cannot guarantee exact convergence under Assumptions
233   1-3. We first illustrate the insight behind the result, then present its formal description.
234
235
236
237
238
239
240
241
242

247   Figure 2: An illustration of the insight on why GaLore fails to converge in small-gradient scenarios. We use
      color green for true gradient and red for gradient noise.
248
249
      Insight behind non-convergence. As reviewed in Sec. 2, GaLore performs SVD on stochastic gra-
250
      dient G = U ОЈV вҠӨ and obtains rank-r projection matrices P = U [:, : r] вҲҲ RmГ—r . GaLore projects
251   G onto P , yielding a low-rank gradient representation P вҠӨ G вҲҲ RrГ—n . In other words, GaLore
252   projects the stochastic gradient matrix onto a low-rank subspace spanned by the top r singular vec-
253   tors, capturing the dominant components of the stochastic gradient matrix. However, the stochastic
254   gradient comprises two components: the true gradient and gradient noise, as shown in Fig. 2. When
255   the true gradient significantly exceeds the gradient noise, typically at the start of training, the low-
256   rank subspace obtained via SVD effectively preserves the true gradient information. As training
257   progresses and the true gradient diminishes to zero, especially near a local minimum, the subspace
258   may become increasingly influenced by gradient noise. In the extreme case, this noise-dominated
259
      subspace can become orthogonal to the true gradient subspace, leading to non-convergence.
260   Counter-Example. We consider the following quadratic problem with gradient noise:
261
                                1
262                    f (X) = вҲҘAXвҲҘ2F + вҹЁB, Xвҹ©F , вҲҮF (X; Оҫ) = вҲҮf (X) + ОҫПғC,                             (1)
                                2

      where A = (InвҲ’r 0) вҲҲ R(nвҲ’r)Г—n , B =                    вҲҲ RnГ—n with D вҲҲ R(nвҲ’r)Г—(nвҲ’r) generated

      randomly, C =               вҲҲ RnГ—n , Оҫ is a random variable uniformly sampled from {1, вҲ’1} per

269   (1) satisfies Assumptions 1-3. Moreover, as X approaches the global minimum of f (X), the true
      gradient вҲҮf (X) вҶ’ 0, while the gradient noise persists with a variance on the order of Пғ 2 . Fig. 1

274
      theorem regarding the non-convergence of GaLore.
275   Theorem 1 (Non-convergence of GaLore). There exists an objective function f : Rd вҶ’ R satisfying
276   Assumptions 1, 2, a stochastic gradient oracle (F, D) satisfying Assumption 3, an initial point x(0) вҲҲ
277   Rd , a constant Пө0 > 0 such that for any rank rв„“ < min{mв„“ , nв„“ }, subspace changing frequency П„ ,
278
      any subspace optimizer ПҒ inputting subspace gradient of shape rв„“ Г— nв„“ and outputting subspace
      update direction of shape rв„“ Г— nв„“ with arbitrary hyperparameters and any t > 0, it holds that

      4   C ONDITIONS UNDER WHICH G A L ORE CAN C ONVERGE

      GaLore provably converges in the noise-free setting. According to the insight presented in Sec. 3,
285   GaLore fails to converge when gradient noise dominates the true gradient in magnitudes. This
286   motivates us to examine the deterministic scenario where the true gradient вҲҮf (x) can be accessed
287   without any gradient noise. The GaLore algorithm with noise-free gradients is presented in Alg. 1 (or
288   Alg. 2 in Appendix B.3), where the true gradient oracle is highlighted with the label (deterministic) .
289                                                                (t)
      Since no gradient noise exists, the projection matrix Pв„“ obtained by SVD can effectively capture
290
      the true gradient even when the algorithm approaches a local minimum. For simplicity, we analyze
291
      GaLore with MSGD and the following momentum updating mechanism:

299   reduces to regular momentum updates. If the subspace changes at iteration t, we inherit Mв„“     by
300   first projecting back to the previous space and then to the new subspace. For convenience, we use
301   momentum projection (MP) to refer to mechanism (2). When MP is used in the algorithm, we label
302   the corresponding with (with MP) in Alg. 1 otherwise (without MP) . The following theorem
303   provides convergence guarantees for GaLore using deterministic gradients and MSGD with MP.
304   Theorem 2 (Convergence rate of deterministic GaLore). Under Assumptions 1-2, if the number of
305   iterations T вүҘ 64/(3Оҙ) and we choose

371   This convergence can be ensured by reducing the gradient noise through an increased batch size, par-
372   ticularly as the algorithm approaches a local minimum. Specifically, we replace the stochastic gra-
               (t)                                                  (t)     PB
373   dient Gв„“ = вҲҮв„“ F (x(t) ; Оҫ (t) ) with large-batch gradient Gв„“ = B1 b=1 вҲҮв„“ F (x(t) ; Оҫ (t,b) ), which
374   reduces the variance of gradient noise by B times. The GaLore algorithm with large-batch stochastic
375   gradients is presented in Alg. 1 (or Alg. 3 in Appendix B.4), where the large-batch stochastic gradi-
376   ent oracle is highlighted with the label (large-batch) . It is worth noting that the non-convergence
377   of GaLore primarily stems from the erroneous subspace dominated by gradient noise. Therefore,
      we compute a large-batch gradient only for the SVD step while maintaining a smaller batch size for

      other computations, see Alg. 1. As the batch size B increases with iteration T , GaLore provably
379   converge to stationary solutions, as established in the following theorem:

392   Remark. A more detailed result is presented in Theorem 7 in Appendix B.4, whereвҲҡ we established
393   convergence for more general hyperparameter choices. The batch size B = Оҳ( T ) in large-batch
394   GaLore grows with iteration T , leading to increased memory overhead, making it less practical
395   than small-batch GaLore. With gradient accumulation, an additional variable is needed to track the
396   gradient, complicating compatibility with per-layer weight updates. Otherwise, larger batch sizes
397   raise the memory required for activation values. Therefore, exploring algorithms that can converge
398   with standard small-batch stochastic gradients becomes essential.
399   Empirical validation. Fig. 1 illustrates the convergence of large-batch GaLore (blue curve) in
400   solving problem (1). It demonstrates that large-batch GaLore effectively corrects the bias present in
401   small-batch stochastic GaLore (green curve), achieving convergence to stationary solutions.
402
      GaLore provably converges with isotropic noise assumptions. In Appendix G, we further prove
403   that under some additional isotropic noise assumptions, GaLore with small-batch stochastic gradi-
404
                                                               вҲҡ
      ents can also be guaranteed to converge at a rate of O(1/ T ).
405
406
407   5   G O L ORE : G RADIENT RANDOM LOW- RANK PROJECTION
408
409
      GoLore algorithm. The main issue with SVD-based projection in GaLore is that it aims to capture
      the dominant component in the stochastic gradient matrix. Consequently, when gradient noise over-
410
      shadows the true gradient as the algorithm approaches a local minimum, the SVD-based projection
411
      fails to identify valuable gradient information.
412
413   To address this, we propose replacing the SVD-based projection with a random projection, which
414   captures components of the stochastic gradient matrix randomly without any preference. This re-
415
      sults in the GoLore algorithm presented in Alg. 1 (or Alg. 4 in Appendix B.5). In Alg. 1, the
                                                                                         (t)
416   GaLore method highlighted with the label (GaLore) samples the projection matrix Pв„“ via SVD
                                                                                                        (t)
417   decomposition. In contrast, the GoLore method highlighted with the label (GoLore) samples Pв„“
418   from U(Stmв„“ ,rв„“ ), a uniform distribution on the mв„“ Г— rв„“ Stiefel manifold. The following proposition
419   provides a practical strategy to sample from distribution U(Stm,r ).
420
      Proposition 1 (Chikuse (2012), Theorem 2.2.1). A random matrix X uniformly distributed on Stm,r
421
      is expressed as X = Z(Z вҠӨ Z)вҲ’1/2 , where the elements of an m Г— r random matrix Z are inde-
422
      pendent and identically distributed as normal N (0, 1).
423
424   Convergence guarantee. Unlike SVD used in GaLore, the random sampling strategy in GoLore
425   prevents the subspace from being dominated by gradient noise. The theorem below provides con-
426   vergence guarantees for GoLore when using small-batch stochastic gradients and MSGD with MP.
427   Theorem 4 вҲҡ (Convergence rate of GoLore). Under Assumptions 1-3, for any T вүҘ 2 + 128/(3Оҙ) +
428   (128Пғ)2 /(9 ОҙLвҲҶ), if we choose П„ = вҢҲ64/(3ОҙОІ1 )вҢү,
429            пЈ«     s            пЈ¶вҲ’1                     s           s             s         !вҲ’1
                          3/2 2                                  2           2 L2           2
430                     Оҙ    Пғ  T                            80L         80П„          16П„ L
         ОІ1 = пЈӯ1 +                пЈё , and О· = 4L +                 +              +               ,
431                        LвҲҶ                                3ОҙОІ12         3Оҙ          3ОІ1

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

                                                                                 вҲҡ
444   Remark. Theorem 4 demonstrates that GaLore converges at a rate of O(1/ T ), which is consistent
445   with the convergence rate of full-parameter pre-training using standard MSGD. A more detailed
446   result is presented in Theorem 8 in Appendix B.5, where we established convergence for more
447   general hyperparameter choices. Unlike deterministic GaLore and low-rank GaLore discussed in
448   Sec. 4, the newly-proposed GoLore algorithm converges in the non-convex stochastic setting with
449   regular batch sizes, making it far more suitable for LLM pre-training and fine-tuning.
450   Practical application of GoLore in LLMs. While GoLore have theoretical convergence guaran-
451   tees, directly applying GoLore in LLM tasks may not be ideal. The advantage of using randomly
452   sampled projection matrices becomes evident in the later stages of training, where stochastic gra-
453   dients are primarily dominated by gradient noise. However, in the early stages, projection matrices
454   derived from SVD retain more gradient information, leading to more effective subspaces. Therefore,
455   we recommend a hybrid approach: initially using GaLore to converge toward the neighborhood of
456
      the solution, then switching to GoLore for refinement and achieving more accurate results.
457   Empirical validation. Fig. 1 shows the convergence of the hybrid algorithm (red curve) applied to
458   problem (1), which employs GaLore during the early training phase and switches to GoLore in the
459   later stage. It is observed that the hybrid algorithm successfully converges to stationary solutions.
460
      6   C ONNECTION WITH OTHER S UBSPACE O PTIMIZATION M ETHODS
461
462   Connection with ReLoRA. Algorithms like GaLore/GoLore that optimizes in periodically recom-
463
      puted subspaces can be implemented in an equivalent yet potentially more computational efficient,
      ReloRA-like way. Consider a linear layer y = W x with W вҲҲ RmГ—n , where m вүӨ n, GaLore first
464
      computes the full-parameter gradient вҲҮW L = (вҲҮy L)xвҠӨ via back propagation and update W in
465
      the subspace as W вҶҗ W + P ПҒ(P вҠӨ (вҲҮW L)), where P вҲҲ RmГ—r is a low-rank projection matrix.
466
      If we use LoRA adaptation W = W0 + BA with B вҲҲ RmГ—r and A вҲҲ RrГ—n , we compute AвҖҷs
467   gradient вҲҮA L = (вҲҮz L)xвҠӨ = B вҠӨ (вҲҮy L)xвҠӨ , where z = Bx is the additional activation. If we fix
468
      B = P , update A вҶҗ A + ПҒ(вҲҮA L) is equivalent to W вҶҗ W + P ПҒ(P вҠӨ (вҲҮW L)). The memory
469   and computational costs of the two implementations are compared in Table 1, showing the potential
470   of our ReLoRA-like implementation to reduce computation with little memory overhead. Detailed
471   algorithm descriptions and calculations are in Appendix D.
472
      Connection with F LORA. Aware of the equivalence of the two (GaLore/ReLoRA-like) implemen-
473
      tations, the main difference between GoLore and F LORA lies in the choice of projection matrices.
474
      Though both algorithms sample P вҲҲ RmГ—r randomly, GoLore uses a uniform distribution on the
475   Stiefel manifold U(Stm,r ), while F LORA uses a random Gaussian distribution where each element
476   in P is independently sampled from N (0, 1/r), and thus P may not belongs to Stm,r .
477
478
      Connection with SIFT. SIFT fine-tunes LLMs with sparsified gradients, which can also be viewed
      as subspace descent. While GaLore projects gradient G to P вҠӨ G via a projection matrix P , SIFT
479
      projects gradient G to S вҠҷ G via a sparse mask matrix S. Our theoretical analysis can be directly
480
      transferred to sparse subspace descent with little effort, implying similar results as in low-rank sub-
481   space descent, see Appendix C.
482

510
511
      GaLoreвҖҷs non-convergence. To validate the non-convergence of GaLore and the convergence prop-
      erties of GoLore and large-batch GaLore, we compare them with full-parameter training on the
512
      constructed quadratic problem defined in (1). Fig. 1 shows that, regardless of whether AdamW or
513
      MSGD is employed as the subspace optimizer, GaLore does not converge to the desired solution.
514   In contrast, both GoLore and large-batch GaLore, along with full-parameter training, achieve exact
515   convergence, thereby validating our theoretical results.
516
517
      Pre-training. To validate the efficiency of GoLore in LLM pre-training tasks, we pre-trained
      LLaMA-60M on the C4 (Raffel et al., 2020) dataset for 10,000 iterations using various algorithms,
518
      including GaLore, GoLore and full-parameter training. All implementations utilized the AdamW
519
      optimizer in BF16 format. As illustrated in Fig. 3, there is a noticeable performance gap between
520   GaLore/GoLore and full-parameter training, indicating that the parameters are away from local min-
521   ima. However, GoLore still demonstrates slightly better training performance compared to GaLore.
522
523
      Fine-tuning. To validate the efficiency of GoLore in LLM fine-tuning tasks, we fine-tuned pre-
      trained LLaMA2-7B models (Touvron et al., 2023) on the WinoGrande dataset (Sakaguchi et al.,
524
      2021) and pre-trained RoBERTa models (Liu, 2019) on the GLUE benchmark (Wang, 2018) with
525
      AdamW optimizers. Fig. 4 displays the fine-tuning loss curves for GaLore and GoLore with rank
526   1024, while Table 2 presents the task scores for GaLore/GoLore and F LORA with rank 4. In both
527   experiments, GoLore outperforms GaLore.
528
529
530   8    C ONCLUSION AND L IMITATIONS
531
      This paper investigates subspace optimization approaches for LLM pre-training and fine-tuning.
532
      We demonstrate that GaLore fails to converge to the desired solution under regular assumptions,
533
      as the SVD-based projection often generates noise-dominated subspaces when the true gradient is
534   relatively small. However, we establish that GaLore can achieve exact convergence when using
535   deterministic or large-batch stochastic gradients. We further introduce GoLoreвҖ”a variant of Ga-
536   Lore employing randomly sampled projection matricesвҖ”and establish its convergence rate even
537   with small-batch stochastic gradients. A limitation of this paper is that convergence guarantees for
538   GoLore are currently provided only when using MSGD as the subspace optimizer. Although GoLore
539   with AdamW performs well empirically, as shown in Table 2, its theoretical convergence guarantees
      remain unknown and will be addressed in future work.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

702
                                                       A PPENDIX
703
704
705   A      C HALLENGES IN THEORETICAL ANALYSIS
706
707   Gradient projection onto a low-rank subspace poses two significant challenges for the convergence
708
      analysis of (momentum) stochastic gradient descent:
709       вҖў Neither unbiased nor contractive compression. gradient projection onto this subspace can
710         be viewed as gradient compression. Traditional analyses of optimization algorithms with lossy
711         compression typically rely on either unbiased (Li et al., 2020; Li & RichtaМҒrik, 2021; Huang &
712         Pu, 2023; He et al., 2024a;b; Condat et al., 2024) compressibility, i.e., the compressor C satisfies
713
                                     E[C(x)] = x,     E[вҲҘC(x) вҲ’ xвҲҘ22 ] вүӨ ПүвҲҘxвҲҘ22 ,       вҲҖx вҲҲ Rd ,
714
715         for some Пү вүҘ 0, or contractive (RichtaМҒrik et al., 2021; Xie et al., 2020; Fatkhullin et al., 2024;
716         He et al., 2023) compressibility, i.e.,
717                                        E[вҲҘC(x) вҲ’ xвҲҘ22 ] вүӨ (1 вҲ’ Оҙ)вҲҘxвҲҘ22 ,     вҲҖx вҲҲ Rd ,
718
            for some Оҙ вҲҲ (0, 1]. However, GaLoreвҖҷs subspace compression is neither unbiased nor contrac-
719         tive due to the reuse of projection matrices. For example, consider a pre-computed projection
720         matrix P вҲҲ RmГ—r . There exists a full-parameter gradient G вҲҲ RmГ—n such that G Мё= 0 and
721         C(G) := P P вҠӨ G = 0, violating both unbiased and contractive compressibility.
722
          вҖў Periodically projected optimizer states. When GaLore changes the subspace, the retained mo-
723         mentum terms must be adjusted to track the gradients in the new subspace. Since these momen-
724         tum terms were initially aligned with the gradients in the original subspace, such adjustments
725         inevitably introduce additional errors, especially when the two subspaces differ significantly.
726         In the extreme case where the two subspaces are entirely orthogonal, the momentum from the
727         previous subspace becomes largely irrelevant for optimization in the new one.
728
729   B      T HEORETICAL PROOFS
730

       GaLore/GoLore to sparse subspace optimization, we still include proofs for completeness.

1821
1822   C.1   A LGORITHM DESIGN
1823
1824   While low-rank subspace optimization algorithms like GaLore/GoLore project full-parameter gra-
1825   dient G вҲҲ R(mГ—n) into low-rank subspaces via projection like P вҠӨ G, sparse subspace optimization
1826   algorithms use a sparse mask S to get S вҠҷ G. Specifically, consider the following set
1827
1828                                Spkm,n = {S вҲҲ {0, 1}mГ—n | вҲҘSвҲҘ2F = k},
1829
1830   i.e., a set of m Г— n matrices contains k ones and (mn вҲ’ k) zeros. Corresponding to the subspace
1831   selecting strategy in GaLore, we consider a Top-k strategy which places the k ones at indices cor-
1832   responding to GвҖҷs elements with the k largest absolute values. We also consider a Rand-k strategy
1833   which samples the sparse mask matrix S from the uniform distribution on SPkm,n corresponding to
1834   GoLore. For convenience, we name the algorithm using Top-k strategy as GaSare (Gradient Sparse
1835   projection), and the one using Rand-k strategy as GoSare (Gradient random Sparse projection). The
       concerned sparse subspace descent algorithms are described as in Alg. 5


                                                           34
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1836

2417
2418   D    T HE R E L O RA- LIKE IMPLEMENTATION
2419
2420   An equivalent, ReLoRA-like implementation of Alg. 1 is as illustrated in Alg. 6, where we only
2421
       present the case with small-batch stochastic gradients for convenience. In fact, applying ReLoRA
       with a fixed A or B is not our contribution, as it has already been used in several previous works(Hao
2422
       et al., 2024; Loeschcke et al., 2024). While leading to the same results, this ReLoRA-like imple-
2423
       mentation (Alg. 6) can potentially save computation as it computes the subspace gradient directly
2424   without computing the full-parameter one. Consider the case where m вүӨ n and we use MSGD and
2425   a batch size of b. The computation complexity of GaLoreвҖҷs original implementation is 2bmn for
2426   forward propagation, 4bmn for backward propagation, 4rmn for projection, 3rn for momentum
2427   update and 2mn for weight update. The computational complexity of our ReLoRA-like imple-
2428   mentation is 2bmn + 2brm + 2brn for forward propagation, 2bmn + 2brm + 2brn for backward
2429   propagation, 3rn for momentum updates and 2rn for weight updates. As illustrated in Table 1, our
       implementation can potentially reduce computation with little memory overhead.

       In this section, we elaborate on the missing details concerned with the experiments we present in
       Sec. 7.
2488
2489   GaLoreвҖҷs non-convergence. We compared Galore, large-batch GaLore, GoLore and full-parameter
2490   training on the constructed quadratic problem defined in (1). We used a batch size of 128 for large-
2491   batch GaLore and a batch size of 1 for the others.
2492   Pre-training tasks on C4 dataset. We pre-trained LLaMA-60M on C4 dataset for 10,000 iterations
2493   on 4 NVIDIA A100 40G GPUs. We use batch size 128, learning rate 1.0e-3, rank 128, scaling factor
2494   Оұ = 1, subspace changing frequency П„ = 200, and a max sequence length of 256. Results under
2495   8-bit training are shown in Fig. 6. Fig. 7 presents the results of different algorithms after trained on
2496   more tokens.

2566   F    C ONNECTIONS WITH OTHER ALGORITHMS
2567
2568   Connection with zero-th order methods. Zero-th order methods (Malladi et al., 2023; Zhang
2569   et al., 2023; Chen et al., 2024) are another line of works on memory-efficient training. While these
2570   algorithms randomly select a direction to estimate the directional derivatives by finite difference,
2571   GoLore computes subspace gradients via back propagation. The directions used in zero-th order
2572
       methods change every iteration, while GoLore applies a more lazily strategy changing its subspace
       every П„ iterations.
2573
2574   Connection with gradient sketching methods. Gradient sketching methods like Hanzely et al.
2575   (2018) and Wang et al. (2024) uses gradient sketches in algorithm iterates. These methods recover
2576   gradient estimates from projected gradients and retains full-size gradients and optimizer states. In
2577   comparison, GoLore directly updates with projected gradients and retains compressed gradients and
2578
       optimizer states, which is more memory-efficient.
2579
2580   G    C ONVERGENCE OF G A L ORE UNDER ISOTROPIC NOISE ASSUMPTIONS
2581
2582   Based on the anisotropic gradient noise we use to construct the counter-example in the proof of
2583   GaLoreвҖҷs non-convergence under standard assumptions, an interesting open question is whether
2584   GaLore is guaranteed to converge if the noise are further assumed isotropic. In this section, we
2585   consider the following additional assumption:
2586   Assumption 4 (Isotropic noise). The distribution of stochastic noise for each gradient matrix is
2587   invariant under orthogonal transformations, i.e., it holds for any layer в„“ = 1, В· В· В· , NL , parameter
2588   x вҲҲ Rd and orthogonal matrix O1 вҲҲ Rmв„“ Г—mв„“ , O2 вҲҲ Rnв„“ Г—nв„“ that

2591            dist
       where A = B represents A and B shares the same distribution.
